Systematic liquidity risk, which correlates with security pricing, influences yield spreads, particularly during volatile market conditions. Friewald et al. (2012) highlighted that liquidity accounts for a significant portion of yield spreads, increasing during financial stress. This effect is noted across all bonds except AAA-rated ones.                   "url": "https://www.investopedia.com/articles/basics/06/invertedyieldcurve.asp",
                  "title": "The Impact of an Inverted Yield Curve",
                  "snippet": "An inverted yield curve occurs when short-term interest rates surpass long-term rates, signaling potential economic downturns or recessions. Under normal conditions, long-term bonds offer higher yields due to greater risk and opportunity costs. However, when investors perceive higher near-term risks, they flock to long-term securities, reducing their yields and potentially inverting the curve. Historically, yield curve inversions have preceded every U.S. recession since 1955, although not all inversions guarantee recessions, as seen before the COVID-19 pandemic.\n\nThe inverted curve affects different economic sectors. Consumers with adjustable-rate loans face higher payments, reducing disposable income and economic activity. Investors may prefer short-term or U.S. Treasury-backed instruments over riskier assets, as returns converge. Companies, especially banks and hedge funds relying on short-long borrowing cycles, may see shrinking profit margins. Defensive sectors like food and healthcare typically fare better during inversion-induced downturns.\n\nThe most recent and longest inversion began on July 5, 2022, and lasted until August 27, 2024. The phenomenon is influenced by factors like foreign demand for U.S. debt and Federal Reserve interest rate hikes. Overall, the yield curve remains a key economic indicator, despite criticisms and limitations in its predictive power.",
